	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Constantinople</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">capital city of the Eastern Roman or Byzantine Empire, the Latin and the Ottoman Empire</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the historical city from 330 to 1453. For its predecessor in Greek and early Roman times, see <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantium</a>. For Constantinople during the Ottoman era and the modern city, see <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Constantinople_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Constantinople (disambiguation)">Constantinople (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Constantinopolis" and "Konstantinopolis" redirect here. For the town of that name in ancient Osrhoene, see <a href="/wiki/Constantinopolis_in_Osrhoene" class="mw-redirect" title="Constantinopolis in Osrhoene">Constantinopolis in Osrhoene</a>. For the Ottoman Greek newspaper, see <a href="/wiki/Konstantinoupolis_(newspaper)" title="Konstantinoupolis (newspaper)">Konstantinoupolis (newspaper)</a>.</div>

<p><b>Constantinople</b> <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="/ɒ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;body&#39;">ɒ</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="/æ/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;bad&#39;">æ</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="/ɪ/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">ɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/oʊ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;code&#39;">oʊ</span><span title="&#39;p&#39; in &#39;pie&#39;">p</span><span title="/əl/: &#39;le&#39; in &#39;bottle&#39;">əl</span></span>/</a></span></span><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">Κωνσταντινούπολις</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Greek" title="Romanization of Greek">translit.</a></small>&#160;<i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek-language romanization"><i>Kōnstantinoúpolis</i></i> (<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:EL-%CE%9A%CF%89%CE%BD%CF%83%CF%84%CE%B1%CE%BD%CF%84%CE%B9%CE%BD%CE%BF%CF%8D%CF%80%CE%BF%CE%BB%CE%B9%CF%82.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/02/EL-%CE%9A%CF%89%CE%BD%CF%83%CF%84%CE%B1%CE%BD%CF%84%CE%B9%CE%BD%CE%BF%CF%8D%CF%80%CE%BF%CE%BB%CE%B9%CF%82.ogg" class="internal" title="EL-Κωνσταντινούπολις.ogg">pronunciation</a></span>&#160;<small class="metadata audiolinkinfo" style="cursor:help;">(<a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Media help"><span style="cursor:help;">help</span></a>·<a href="/wiki/File:EL-%CE%9A%CF%89%CE%BD%CF%83%CF%84%CE%B1%CE%BD%CF%84%CE%B9%CE%BD%CE%BF%CF%8D%CF%80%CE%BF%CE%BB%CE%B9%CF%82.ogg" title="File:EL-Κωνσταντινούπολις.ogg"><span style="cursor:help;">info</span></a>)</small></span>); <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">Cōnstantīnopolis</i>) was the <a href="/wiki/Capital_city" title="Capital city">capital city</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> (330–395), of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Roman_(Byzantine)_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Roman (Byzantine) Empire">Eastern Roman (Byzantine) Empire</a> (395–1204 and 1261–1453), of the brief <a href="/wiki/Crusader_states#Fourth_Crusade" title="Crusader states">Crusader state</a> known as the <a href="/wiki/Latin_Empire" title="Latin Empire">Latin Empire</a> (1204–1261) and of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> (1453–1923). In 1923 the capital of <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, the successor state of the Ottoman Empire, was moved to <a href="/wiki/Ankara" title="Ankara">Ankara</a> and the name Constantinople was officially changed to <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>. The city is located in what is now the European side and the core of modern Istanbul. The city is still referred to as Constantinople in Greek-speaking sources.
</p><p>In 324 ancient <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantium</a> became the new capital of the Roman Empire by Emperor <a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great" title="Constantine the Great">Constantine the Great</a>, after whom it was renamed, and dedicated on 11 May 330.<sup id="cite_ref-ODB_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODB-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> From the mid-5th century to the early 13th century, Constantinople was the largest and wealthiest city in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The city became famous for its architectural masterpieces, such as <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a>, the cathedral of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a>, which served as the seat of the <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarchate_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople">Ecumenical Patriarchate</a>, the sacred <a href="/wiki/Great_Palace_of_Constantinople" title="Great Palace of Constantinople">Imperial Palace</a> where the Emperors lived, the <a href="/wiki/Galata_Tower" title="Galata Tower">Galata Tower</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hippodrome_of_Constantinople" title="Hippodrome of Constantinople">Hippodrome</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Gate_(Constantinople)" class="mw-redirect" title="Golden Gate (Constantinople)">Golden Gate</a> of the Land Walls, and the opulent aristocratic palaces lining the arcaded avenues and squares. The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Constantinople" title="University of Constantinople">University of Constantinople</a> was founded in the fifth century and contained numerous artistic and literary treasures before it was sacked in 1204 and 1453,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> including its vast <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Library_of_Constantinople" title="Imperial Library of Constantinople">Imperial Library</a> which contained the remnants of the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Alexandria" title="Library of Alexandria">Library of Alexandria</a> and had over 100,000 volumes of ancient texts.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> It was instrumental in the advancement of Christianity during Roman and Byzantine times as the home of the <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarch_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarch of Constantinople">Ecumenical Patriarch of Constantinople</a> and as the guardian of Christendom's holiest relics such as the <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_thorns" title="Crown of thorns">Crown of thorns</a> and the <a href="/wiki/True_Cross" title="True Cross">True Cross</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bizansist_touchup.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Bizansist_touchup.jpg/300px-Bizansist_touchup.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="116" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Bizansist_touchup.jpg/450px-Bizansist_touchup.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Bizansist_touchup.jpg/600px-Bizansist_touchup.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2516" data-file-height="970" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bizansist_touchup.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aerial view of Byzantine Constantinople and the Propontis (Sea of Marmara).</div></div></div>
<p>Constantinople was famed for its massive and complex defences. The first wall of the city was erected by <a href="/wiki/Constantine_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Constantine I">Constantine I</a>, and surrounded the city on both land and sea fronts. Later, in the 5th century, the <a href="/wiki/Praetorian_prefect" title="Praetorian prefect">Praetorian prefect</a> <a href="/wiki/Anthemius_(praetorian_prefect)" title="Anthemius (praetorian prefect)">Anthemius</a> under the child emperor <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_II" title="Theodosius II">Theodosius II</a> undertook the construction of the <a href="/wiki/Theodosian_Walls" class="mw-redirect" title="Theodosian Walls">Theodosian Walls</a>, which consisted of a double wall lying about 2 kilometres (1.2&#160;mi) to the west of the first wall and a moat with palisades in front.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> This formidable complex of defences was one of the most sophisticated of <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">Antiquity</a>. The city was built intentionally to rival Rome, and it was claimed that several elevations within its walls matched the 'seven hills' of Rome. Because it was located between the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Horn" title="Golden Horn">Golden Horn</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Marmara" title="Sea of Marmara">Sea of Marmara</a> the land area that needed defensive walls was reduced, and this helped it to present an impregnable fortress enclosing magnificent palaces, domes, and towers, the result of the prosperity it achieved from being the gateway between two continents (Europe and Asia) and two seas (the Mediterranean and the Black Sea). Although besieged on numerous occasions by various armies, the defences of Constantinople proved impregnable for nearly nine hundred years.
</p><p>In 1204, however, the armies of the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Crusade" title="Fourth Crusade">Fourth Crusade</a> took and devastated the city, and its inhabitants lived several decades under Latin misrule.  In 1261 the Byzantine Emperor <a href="/wiki/Michael_VIII_Palaiologos" title="Michael VIII Palaiologos">Michael VIII Palaiologos</a> liberated the city, and after the restoration under the <a href="/wiki/Palaiologos" title="Palaiologos">Palaiologos</a> dynasty, enjoyed a partial recovery.  With the advent of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> in 1299, the Byzantine Empire began to lose territories and the city began to lose population. By the early 15th century, the Byzantine Empire was reduced to just Constantinople and its environs, along with <a href="/wiki/Morea" title="Morea">Morea</a> in Greece, making it an enclave inside the Ottoman Empire; after a 53-day siege the city eventually <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">fell to the Ottomans</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Sultan" title="Sultan">Sultan</a> <a href="/wiki/Mehmed_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Mehmed II">Mehmed II</a>, on 29 May 1453,<sup id="cite_ref-mw28_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mw28-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> whereafter it replaced <a href="/wiki/Edirne" title="Edirne">Edirne</a> (Adrianople) as the new capital of the Ottoman Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Names">Names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Names">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Before_Constantinople">Before Constantinople</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Before Constantinople">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)">Natural History</a></i>, the first known name of a settlement on the site of Constantinople was <i>Lygos</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> a settlement likely of <a href="/wiki/Thracian" class="mw-redirect" title="Thracian">Thracian</a> origin founded between the 13th and 11th centuries BC.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The site, according to the founding myth of the city, was abandoned by the time Greek settlers from the city-state of <a href="/wiki/Megara" title="Megara">Megara</a> founded <i><a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantium</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">Βυζάντιον</span>, <i>Byzántion</i>) in around 657&#160;BC,<sup id="cite_ref-roo177_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-roo177-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> across from the town of <a href="/wiki/Chalcedon" title="Chalcedon">Chalcedon</a> on the Asiatic side of the Bosphorus.
</p><p>The origins of the name of <i><a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantion</a></i>, more commonly known by the later Latin <i>Byzantium</i>, are not entirely clear, though some suggest it is of Thraco-<a href="/wiki/Illyria" title="Illyria">Illyrian</a> origin.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The founding myth of the city has it told that the settlement was named after the leader of the Megarian colonists, Byzas. The later Byzantines of Constantinople themselves would maintain that the city was named in honour of two men, Byzas and Antes, though this was more likely just a play on the word <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The city was briefly renamed <i>Augusta Antonina</i> in the early 3rd century AD by the Emperor <a href="/wiki/Septimius_Severus" title="Septimius Severus">Septimius Severus</a> (193–211), who razed the city to the ground in 196 for supporting a <a href="/wiki/Pescennius_Niger" title="Pescennius Niger">rival contender</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Year_of_the_Five_Emperors" title="Year of the Five Emperors">civil war</a> and had it rebuilt in honour of his son Marcus Aurelius Antoninus (who succeeded him as Emperor), popularly known as <a href="/wiki/Caracalla" title="Caracalla">Caracalla</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IA_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IA-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The name appears to have been quickly forgotten and abandoned, and the city reverted to Byzantium/Byzantion after either the assassination of Caracalla in 217 or, at the latest, the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Severan_dynasty" title="Severan dynasty">Severan dynasty</a> in 235.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Names_of_Constantinople">Names of Constantinople</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Names of Constantinople">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Names_of_Constantinople" class="mw-redirect" title="Names of Constantinople">Names of Constantinople</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Keystone_Constantine_Forum_Istanbul.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Keystone_Constantine_Forum_Istanbul.JPG/220px-Keystone_Constantine_Forum_Istanbul.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Keystone_Constantine_Forum_Istanbul.JPG/330px-Keystone_Constantine_Forum_Istanbul.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Keystone_Constantine_Forum_Istanbul.JPG/440px-Keystone_Constantine_Forum_Istanbul.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Keystone_Constantine_Forum_Istanbul.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This huge <a href="/wiki/Keystone_(architecture)" title="Keystone (architecture)">keystone</a> found in <a href="/wiki/%C3%87emberlita%C5%9F,_Fatih" title="Çemberlitaş, Fatih">Çemberlitaş, Fatih</a> might have belonged to a <a href="/wiki/Triumphal_arch" title="Triumphal arch">triumphal arch</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Forum_of_Constantine" title="Forum of Constantine">Forum of Constantine</a>; the forum was built by <a href="/wiki/Constantine_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Constantine I">Constantine I</a> in the quarter of modern-day Çemberlitaş.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Column_of_Constantine,_July_2010.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Column_of_Constantine%2C_July_2010.jpg/220px-Column_of_Constantine%2C_July_2010.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Column_of_Constantine%2C_July_2010.jpg/330px-Column_of_Constantine%2C_July_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Column_of_Constantine%2C_July_2010.jpg/440px-Column_of_Constantine%2C_July_2010.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4032" data-file-height="6048" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Column_of_Constantine,_July_2010.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Column_of_Constantine" title="Column of Constantine">Column of Constantine</a>, built by <a href="/wiki/Constantine_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Constantine I">Constantine I</a> in 330 to commemorate the establishment of Constantinople as the <a href="/wiki/New_Rome" title="New Rome">new capital</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Byzantium took on the name of <i>Kōnstantinoupolis</i> ("city of Constantine", <i>Constantinople</i>) after its refoundation under Roman emperor <a href="/wiki/Constantine_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Constantine I">Constantine I</a>, who transferred the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> to Byzantium in 330 and designated his new capital officially as <i><a href="/wiki/New_Rome" title="New Rome">Nova Roma</a></i> (<span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">Νέα Ῥώμη</span>) 'New Rome'. During this time, the city was also called 'Second Rome', 'Eastern Rome', and <i>Roma Constantinopolitana</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> As the city became the sole remaining capital of the Roman Empire after the fall of the West, and its wealth, population, and influence grew, the city also came to have a multitude of nicknames.
</p><p>As the largest and wealthiest city in Europe during the 4th–13th centuries and a centre of culture and education of the Mediterranean basin, Constantinople came to be known by prestigious titles such as <i>Basileuousa</i> (Queen of Cities) and <i>Megalopolis</i> (the Great City) and was, in colloquial speech, commonly referred to as just <i>Polis</i> (<span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">ἡ Πόλις</span>) 'the City' by Constantinopolitans and provincial Byzantines alike.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the language of other peoples, Constantinople was referred to just as reverently. The medieval Vikings, who had contacts with the empire through their expansion in eastern Europe (<a href="/wiki/Varangians" title="Varangians">Varangians</a>) used the Old Norse name <i>Miklagarðr</i> (from <i>mikill</i> 'big' and <i>garðr</i> 'city'), and later <i>Miklagard</i> and <i>Miklagarth</i>. In Arabic, the city was sometimes called <i>Rūmiyyat al-Kubra</i> (Great City of the Romans) and in Persian as <i>Takht-e Rum</i> (Throne of the Romans).
</p><p>In East and South Slavic languages, including in <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus&#39;">medieval Russia</a>, Constantinople has been referred to as <i><a href="/wiki/Tsargrad" title="Tsargrad">Tsargrad</a></i> (<i>Царьград</i>) or <i>Carigrad</i>, 'City of the Caesar (Emperor)', from the Slavonic words <i>tsar</i> ('Caesar' or 'King') and <i>grad</i> ('city'). This was presumably a <a href="/wiki/Calque" title="Calque">calque</a> on a Greek phrase such as <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">Βασιλέως Πόλις</span> (<i>Vasileos Polis</i>), 'the city of the emperor [king]'.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_names_of_the_city">Modern names of the city</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Modern names of the city">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The modern Turkish name for the city, <i><a href="/wiki/%C4%B0stanbul" class="mw-redirect" title="İstanbul">İstanbul</a></i>, derives from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> phrase <i>eis tin polin</i> (<span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">εἰς τὴν πόλιν</span>), meaning "(in)to the city".<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> This name was used in <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a> alongside <i>Kostantiniyye</i>, the more formal adaptation of the original <i>Constantinople</i>, during the period of <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> rule, while western languages mostly continued to refer to the city as Constantinople until the early 20th century. In 1928, <a href="/wiki/Turkish_Language_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="Turkish Language Commission">the Turkish alphabet was changed</a> from Arabic script to Latin script. After that, as part of the 1920s <a href="/wiki/Turkification" title="Turkification">Turkification</a> movement, Turkey started to urge other countries to use <a href="/wiki/Geographical_name_changes_in_Turkey" title="Geographical name changes in Turkey">Turkish names for Turkish cities</a>, instead of other transliterations to Latin script that had been used in Ottoman times.<sup id="cite_ref-shawn_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shawn-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> In time the city came to be known as Istanbul and its variations in most world languages.
</p><p>The name "Constantinople" is still used by members of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a> in the title of one of their most important leaders, the Orthodox <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarch_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarch of Constantinople">patriarch</a> based in the city, referred to as "His Most Divine All-Holiness the Archbishop of Constantinople New Rome and Ecumenical Patriarch." In Greece today, the city is still called <i>Konstantinoúpoli(s)</i> (<span lang="el" title="Greek language text">Κωνσταντινούπολις/Κωνσταντινούπολη</span>) or simply just "the City" (<span lang="el" title="Greek language text">Η Πόλη</span>).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Byzantium_and_earlier_settlements">Byzantium and earlier settlements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Byzantium and earlier settlements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantium</a></div>
<p>Constantinople was founded by the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Emperor" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Emperor">Roman Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Constantine_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Constantine I">Constantine I</a> (272–337) in 324<sup id="cite_ref-ODB_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODB-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> on the site of an already-existing city, <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantium</a>, which was settled in the early days of <a href="/wiki/Greek_colonies" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek colonies">Greek colonial expansion</a>, in around 657 BC, by colonists of the city-state of <a href="/wiki/Megara" title="Megara">Megara</a>. This is the first major settlement that would develop on the site of later Constantinople, but the first known settlements was that of <i>Lygos</i>, referred to in Pliny's Natural Histories.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Apart from this, little is known about this initial settlement. The site, according to the founding myth of the city, was abandoned by the time Greek settlers from the city-state of Megara founded Byzantium (<span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">Βυζάντιον</span>) in around 657&#160;BC,<sup id="cite_ref-IA_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IA-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> across from the town of Chalcedon on the Asiatic side of the Bosphorus.
</p><p>The city maintained independence as a city-state until it was annexed by <a href="/wiki/Darius_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Darius I">Darius I</a> in 512&#160;BC into the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Persian Empire</a>, who saw the site as the optimal location to construct a <a href="/wiki/Pontoon_bridge" title="Pontoon bridge">pontoon bridge</a> crossing into Europe as Byzantium was situated at the narrowest point in the Bosphorus strait. Persian rule lasted until 478&#160;BC when as part of the Greek counterattack to the <a href="/wiki/Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="Second Persian invasion of Greece">Second Persian invasion of Greece</a>, a Greek army led by the Spartan general <a href="/wiki/Pausanias_(general)" title="Pausanias (general)">Pausanias</a> captured the city which remained an independent, yet subordinate, city under the Athenians, and later to the Spartans after 411&#160;BC.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> A farsighted treaty with the emergent power of Rome in <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;150 BC</span> which stipulated tribute in exchange for independent status allowed it to enter Roman rule unscathed.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> This treaty would pay dividends retrospectively as Byzantium would maintain this independent status, and prosper under peace and stability in the <a href="/wiki/Pax_Romana" title="Pax Romana">Pax Romana</a>, for nearly three centuries until the late 2nd century AD.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Byzantium was never a major influential city-state like that of <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corinth" title="Corinth">Corinth</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a>, but the city enjoyed relative peace and steady growth as a prosperous trading city lent by its remarkable position. The site lay astride the land route from <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> to <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Turkish_Straits" title="Turkish Straits">seaway</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a>, and had in the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Horn" title="Golden Horn">Golden Horn</a> an excellent and spacious harbour. Already then, in Greek and early Roman times, Byzantium was famous for its strategic geographic position that made it difficult to besiege and capture, and its position at the crossroads of the Asiatic-European trade route over land and as the gateway between the Mediterranean and Black Seas made it too valuable a settlement to abandon, as Emperor <a href="/wiki/Septimius_Severus" title="Septimius Severus">Septimius Severus</a> later realized when he razed the city to the ground for supporting <a href="/wiki/Pescennius_Niger" title="Pescennius Niger">Pescennius Niger</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Year_of_the_Five_Emperors" title="Year of the Five Emperors">claimancy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> It was a move greatly criticized by the contemporary consul and historian <a href="/wiki/Cassius_Dio" title="Cassius Dio">Cassius Dio</a> who said that Severus had destroyed "a strong Roman outpost and a base of operations against the barbarians from Pontus and Asia".<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> He would later rebuild Byzantium towards the end of his reign, in which it would be briefly renamed <i>Augusta Antonina</i>, fortifying it with a new city wall in his name, the Severan Wall.
</p>
<h3><span id="324.E2.80.93337:_Foundation_of_Constantinople"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="324–337:_Foundation_of_Constantinople">324–337: Foundation of Constantinople</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 324–337: Foundation of Constantinople">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Constantine_I_Hagia_Sophia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Constantine_I_Hagia_Sophia.jpg/220px-Constantine_I_Hagia_Sophia.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Constantine_I_Hagia_Sophia.jpg/330px-Constantine_I_Hagia_Sophia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Constantine_I_Hagia_Sophia.jpg/440px-Constantine_I_Hagia_Sophia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="688" data-file-height="702" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Constantine_I_Hagia_Sophia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Emperor <a href="/wiki/Constantine_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Constantine I">Constantine I</a> presents a representation of the city of Constantinople as tribute to an enthroned Mary and Christ Child in this church mosaic. <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a>, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1000</span>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Urbs_Roma,_commemorative_coin_of_Constantinople.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Commemorative Ancient Coin of Constantinople" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Urbs_Roma%2C_commemorative_coin_of_Constantinople.jpg/220px-Urbs_Roma%2C_commemorative_coin_of_Constantinople.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="102" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Urbs_Roma%2C_commemorative_coin_of_Constantinople.jpg/330px-Urbs_Roma%2C_commemorative_coin_of_Constantinople.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Urbs_Roma%2C_commemorative_coin_of_Constantinople.jpg/440px-Urbs_Roma%2C_commemorative_coin_of_Constantinople.jpg 2x" data-file-width="912" data-file-height="423" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Urbs_Roma,_commemorative_coin_of_Constantinople.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Another coin struck by Constantine I in 330–333 to commemorate the foundation of Constantinople and to also reaffirm Rome as the traditional centre of the Roman Empire.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Constantinopolis_coin.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Constantinopolis_coin.jpg/220px-Constantinopolis_coin.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="109" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Constantinopolis_coin.jpg/330px-Constantinopolis_coin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Constantinopolis_coin.jpg/440px-Constantinopolis_coin.jpg 2x" data-file-width="798" data-file-height="394" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Constantinopolis_coin.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Coin" title="Coin">Coin</a> struck by Constantine I to commemorate the founding of Constantinople.</div></div></div>
<p>Constantine had altogether more colourful plans. Having restored the unity of the Empire, and, being in the course of major governmental reforms as well as of <a href="/wiki/Constantine_I_and_Christianity" class="mw-redirect" title="Constantine I and Christianity">sponsoring the consolidation of the Christian church</a>, he was well aware that Rome was an unsatisfactory capital. Rome was too far from the frontiers, and hence from the armies and the imperial courts, and it offered an undesirable playground for disaffected politicians. Yet it had been the capital of the state for over a thousand years, and it might have seemed unthinkable to suggest that the capital be moved to a different location. Nevertheless, Constantine identified the site of Byzantium as the right place: a place where an emperor could sit, readily defended, with easy access to the <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a> frontiers, his court supplied from the rich gardens and sophisticated workshops of Roman Asia, his treasuries filled by the wealthiest provinces of the Empire.
</p><p>Constantinople was built over six years, and consecrated on 11 May 330.<sup id="cite_ref-ODB_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODB-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Constantine divided the expanded city, like Rome, into 14 regions, and ornamented it with public works worthy of an imperial metropolis.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Yet, at first, Constantine's new Rome did not have all the dignities of old Rome. It possessed a <a href="/wiki/Proconsul" title="Proconsul">proconsul</a>, rather than an <a href="/wiki/Urban_prefect" class="mw-redirect" title="Urban prefect">urban prefect</a>. It had no <a href="/wiki/Praetors" class="mw-redirect" title="Praetors">praetors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tribunes" class="mw-redirect" title="Tribunes">tribunes</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Quaestors" class="mw-redirect" title="Quaestors">quaestors</a>. Although it did have senators, they held the title <i>clarus</i>, not <i><a href="/wiki/Clarissimus" class="mw-redirect" title="Clarissimus">clarissimus</a></i>, like those of Rome. It also lacked the panoply of other administrative offices regulating the food supply, police, statues, temples, sewers, aqueducts, or other public works. The new programme of building was carried out in great haste: columns, marbles, doors, and tiles were taken wholesale from the temples of the empire and moved to the new city. In similar fashion, many of the greatest works of Greek and Roman art were soon to be seen in its squares and streets. The emperor stimulated private building by promising householders gifts of land from the imperial estates in <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Asia" title="Diocese of Asia">Asiana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Pontus" title="Diocese of Pontus">Pontica</a> and on 18 May 332 he announced that, as in Rome, free distributions of food would be made to the citizens. At the time, the amount is said to have been 80,000 rations a day, doled out from 117 distribution points around the city.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Constantine laid out a new square at the centre of old Byzantium, naming it the <a href="/wiki/Augustaion" title="Augustaion">Augustaeum</a>. The new senate-house (or Curia) was housed in a basilica on the east side. On the south side of the great square was erected the <a href="/wiki/Great_Palace_of_Constantinople" title="Great Palace of Constantinople">Great Palace</a> of the Emperor with its imposing entrance, the <a href="/wiki/Chalke" title="Chalke">Chalke</a>, and its ceremonial suite known as the <a href="/wiki/Daphne_Palace" class="mw-redirect" title="Daphne Palace">Palace of Daphne</a>. Nearby was the vast <a href="/wiki/Hippodrome_of_Constantinople" title="Hippodrome of Constantinople">Hippodrome</a> for chariot-races, seating over 80,000 spectators, and the famed <a href="/wiki/Baths_of_Zeuxippus" title="Baths of Zeuxippus">Baths of Zeuxippus</a>. At the western entrance to the Augustaeum was the <a href="/wiki/Milion" title="Milion">Milion</a>, a vaulted monument from which distances were measured across the Eastern Roman Empire.
</p><p>From the Augustaeum led a great street, the <a href="/wiki/Mese_(Constantinople)" title="Mese (Constantinople)">Mese</a> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">Μέση [Οδός]</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a></small>&#160;'"Middle [Street]"'), lined with colonnades. As it descended the First Hill of the city and climbed the Second Hill, it passed on the left the <a href="/wiki/Praetorium" title="Praetorium">Praetorium</a> or law-court. Then it passed through the oval <a href="/wiki/Forum_of_Constantine" title="Forum of Constantine">Forum of Constantine</a> where there was a second Senate-house and a <a href="/wiki/Column_of_Constantine" title="Column of Constantine">high column</a> with a statue of Constantine himself in the guise of <a href="/wiki/Helios" title="Helios">Helios</a>, crowned with a halo of seven rays and looking toward the rising sun. From there, the Mese passed on and through the <a href="/wiki/Forum_Tauri" class="mw-redirect" title="Forum Tauri">Forum Tauri</a> and then the <a href="/wiki/Forum_Bovis" class="mw-redirect" title="Forum Bovis">Forum Bovis</a>, and finally up the Seventh Hill (or Xerolophus) and through to the Golden Gate in the <a href="/wiki/Wall_of_Constantine_(Constantinople)" class="mw-redirect" title="Wall of Constantine (Constantinople)">Constantinian Wall</a>. After the construction of the <a href="/wiki/Theodosian_Walls" class="mw-redirect" title="Theodosian Walls">Theodosian Walls</a> in the early 5th century, it was extended to the new <a href="/wiki/Golden_Gate_(Constantinople)" class="mw-redirect" title="Golden Gate (Constantinople)">Golden Gate</a>, reaching a total length of seven <a href="/wiki/Roman_mile" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman mile">Roman miles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> After the construction of the Theodosian Walls, Constantinople consisted of an area approximately the size of Old Rome within the Aurelian walls, or some 1,400 ha.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBogdanović2016100_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBogdanović2016100-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="337.E2.80.93529:_Constantinople_during_the_Barbarian_Invasions_and_the_fall_of_the_West"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="337–529:_Constantinople_during_the_Barbarian_Invasions_and_the_fall_of_the_West">337–529: Constantinople during the Barbarian Invasions and the fall of the West</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 337–529: Constantinople during the Barbarian Invasions and the fall of the West">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Theodosius_colum,_Istanbul.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Theodosius_colum%2C_Istanbul.jpg/120px-Theodosius_colum%2C_Istanbul.jpg" decoding="async" width="120" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Theodosius_colum%2C_Istanbul.jpg/180px-Theodosius_colum%2C_Istanbul.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Theodosius_colum%2C_Istanbul.jpg/240px-Theodosius_colum%2C_Istanbul.jpg 2x" data-file-width="425" data-file-height="530" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Theodosius_colum,_Istanbul.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Theodosius_I" title="Theodosius I">Theodosius I</a> was the last <a href="/wiki/Roman_emperor" title="Roman emperor">Roman emperor</a> who ruled over an undivided empire (detail from the Obelisk at the <a href="/wiki/Hippodrome_of_Constantinople" title="Hippodrome of Constantinople">Hippodrome of Constantinople</a>).</div></div></div>
<p>The importance of Constantinople increased, but it was gradual. From the death of Constantine in 337 to the accession of Theodosius I, emperors had been resident only in the years 337–338, 347–351, 358–361, 368–369. Its status as a capital was recognized by the appointment of the first known Urban Prefect of the City Honoratus, who held office from 11 December 359 until 361. The urban prefects had concurrent jurisdiction over three provinces each in the adjacent dioceses of Thrace (in which the city was located), Pontus and Asia comparable to the 100-mile extraordinary jurisdiction of the prefect of Rome. The emperor <a href="/wiki/Valens" title="Valens">Valens</a>, who hated the city and spent only one year there, nevertheless built the Palace of <a href="/wiki/Hebdomon" class="mw-redirect" title="Hebdomon">Hebdomon</a> on the shore of the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Marmara" title="Sea of Marmara">Propontis</a> near the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Gate_(Constantinople)" class="mw-redirect" title="Golden Gate (Constantinople)">Golden Gate</a>, probably for use when reviewing troops. All the emperors up to <a href="/wiki/Zeno_(emperor)" title="Zeno (emperor)">Zeno</a> and <a href="/wiki/Basiliscus" title="Basiliscus">Basiliscus</a> were crowned and acclaimed at the Hebdomon. <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_I" title="Theodosius I">Theodosius I</a> founded the <a href="/wiki/Studion" class="mw-redirect" title="Studion">Church of John the Baptist</a> to house the skull of the saint (today preserved at the <a href="/wiki/Topkap%C4%B1_Palace" title="Topkapı Palace">Topkapı Palace</a>), put up a memorial pillar to himself in the Forum of Taurus, and turned the ruined temple of <a href="/wiki/Aphrodite" title="Aphrodite">Aphrodite</a> into a coach house for the <a href="/wiki/Praetorian_prefecture_of_the_East" title="Praetorian prefecture of the East">Praetorian Prefect</a>; <a href="/wiki/Arcadius" title="Arcadius">Arcadius</a> built a new forum named after himself on the Mese, near the walls of Constantine.
</p><p>After the shock of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Adrianople" title="Battle of Adrianople">Battle of Adrianople</a> in 378, in which the emperor <a href="/wiki/Valens" title="Valens">Valens</a> with the flower of the Roman armies was destroyed by the <a href="/wiki/Visigoths" title="Visigoths">Visigoths</a> within a few days' march, the city looked to its defences, and in 413–414 <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_II" title="Theodosius II">Theodosius II</a> built the 18-metre (60-foot)-tall <a href="/wiki/Walls_of_Constantinople" title="Walls of Constantinople">triple-wall fortifications</a>, which were not to be breached until the coming of gunpowder. Theodosius also founded a <a href="/wiki/University_of_Constantinople" title="University of Constantinople">University</a> near the Forum of Taurus, on 27 February 425.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Uldin" title="Uldin">Uldin</a>, a prince of the <a href="/wiki/Huns" title="Huns">Huns</a>, appeared on the Danube about this time and advanced into Thrace, but he was deserted by many of his followers, who joined with the Romans in driving their king back north of the river. Subsequent to this, new walls were built to defend the city and the fleet on the Danube improved.
</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/Barbarian" title="Barbarian">barbarians</a> overran the Western Roman Empire, Constantinople became the indisputable capital city of the Roman Empire. Emperors were no longer peripatetic between various court capitals and palaces. They remained in their palace in the Great City and sent generals to command their armies. The wealth of the eastern Mediterranean and western Asia flowed into Constantinople.
</p>
<h3><span id="527.E2.80.93565:_Constantinople_in_the_Age_of_Justinian"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="527–565:_Constantinople_in_the_Age_of_Justinian">527–565: Constantinople in the Age of Justinian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 527–565: Constantinople in the Age of Justinian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Constantinople_(1422)_by_Florentine_cartographer_Cristoforo_Buondelmonte.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Map_of_Constantinople_%281422%29_by_Florentine_cartographer_Cristoforo_Buondelmonte.jpg/120px-Map_of_Constantinople_%281422%29_by_Florentine_cartographer_Cristoforo_Buondelmonte.jpg" decoding="async" width="120" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Map_of_Constantinople_%281422%29_by_Florentine_cartographer_Cristoforo_Buondelmonte.jpg/180px-Map_of_Constantinople_%281422%29_by_Florentine_cartographer_Cristoforo_Buondelmonte.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Map_of_Constantinople_%281422%29_by_Florentine_cartographer_Cristoforo_Buondelmonte.jpg/240px-Map_of_Constantinople_%281422%29_by_Florentine_cartographer_Cristoforo_Buondelmonte.jpg 2x" data-file-width="592" data-file-height="746" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Constantinople_(1422)_by_Florentine_cartographer_Cristoforo_Buondelmonte.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of Constantinople (1422) by Florentine cartographer <a href="/wiki/Cristoforo_Buondelmonti" title="Cristoforo Buondelmonti">Cristoforo Buondelmonti</a><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> is the oldest surviving map of the city, and the only one that predates the Turkish conquest of the city in 1453.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg/120px-Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg" decoding="async" width="120" height="80" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg/180px-Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg/240px-Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5514" data-file-height="3681" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The current <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a> was commissioned by Emperor <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a> after the previous one was destroyed in the <a href="/wiki/Nika_riots" title="Nika riots">Nika riots</a> of 532. It was converted into a mosque in 1453 when the Ottoman Empire commenced and became a museum in 1935.</div></div></div>
<p>The emperor <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a> (527–565) was known for his successes in war, for his legal reforms and for his public works. It was from Constantinople that his expedition for the reconquest of the former Diocese of Africa set sail on or about 21 June 533. Before their departure, the ship of the commander <a href="/wiki/Belisarius" title="Belisarius">Belisarius</a> was anchored in front of the Imperial palace, and the Patriarch offered prayers for the success of the enterprise. After the victory, in 534, the <a href="/wiki/Herod%27s_Temple" class="mw-redirect" title="Herod&#39;s Temple">Temple treasure of Jerusalem</a>, looted by the Romans in <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Jerusalem_(70)" class="mw-redirect" title="Siege of Jerusalem (70)">AD&#160;70</a> and taken to <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Vandals" title="Vandals">Vandals</a> after their sack of Rome in 455, was brought to Constantinople and deposited for a time, perhaps in the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_St._Polyeuctus" title="Church of St. Polyeuctus">Church of St. Polyeuctus</a>, before being returned to <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem_in_Christianity" title="Jerusalem in Christianity">Jerusalem</a> in either the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre" title="Church of the Holy Sepulchre">Church of the Resurrection</a> or the New Church.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Chariot-racing had been important in Rome for centuries. In Constantinople, the hippodrome became over time increasingly a place of political significance. It was where (as a shadow of the popular elections of old Rome) the people by acclamation showed their approval of a new emperor, and also where they openly criticized the government, or clamoured for the removal of unpopular ministers. In the time of Justinian, public order in Constantinople became a critical political issue.
</p><p>Throughout the late Roman and early Byzantine periods, Christianity was resolving fundamental questions of identity, and the dispute between the <a href="/wiki/Chalcedonian_Orthodoxy" class="mw-redirect" title="Chalcedonian Orthodoxy">orthodox</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Monophysites" class="mw-redirect" title="Monophysites">monophysites</a> became the cause of serious disorder, expressed through allegiance to the horse-racing parties of the Blues and the Greens. The partisans of the Blues and the Greens were said<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> to affect untrimmed facial hair, head hair shaved at the front and grown long at the back, and wide-sleeved tunics tight at the wrist; and to form gangs to engage in night-time muggings and street violence. At last these disorders took the form of a major rebellion of 532, known as the <a href="/wiki/Nika_riots" title="Nika riots">"Nika" riots</a> (from the battle-cry of "Conquer!" of those involved).<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Fires started by the Nika rioters consumed Constantine's basilica of Hagia Sophia (Holy Wisdom), the city's principal church, which lay to the north of the Augustaeum. Justinian commissioned <a href="/wiki/Anthemius_of_Tralles" title="Anthemius of Tralles">Anthemius of Tralles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isidore_of_Miletus" title="Isidore of Miletus">Isidore of Miletus</a> to replace it with a new and incomparable <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a>. This was the great cathedral of the Orthodox Church, whose dome was said to be held aloft by God alone, and which was directly connected to the palace so that the imperial family could attend services without passing through the streets.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The dedication took place on 26 December 537 in the presence of the emperor, who exclaimed, "O <a href="/wiki/Solomon%27s_Temple" title="Solomon&#39;s Temple">Solomon</a>, I have outdone thee!"<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Hagia Sophia was served by 600 people including 80 priests, and cost 20,000 pounds of gold to build.<sup id="cite_ref-cost_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cost-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Justinian also had Anthemius and Isidore demolish and replace the original Church of the Holy Apostles built by Constantine with a <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Holy_Apostles" title="Church of the Holy Apostles">new church</a> under the same dedication. This was designed in the form of an equal-armed cross with five domes, and ornamented with beautiful mosaics. This church was to remain the burial place of the Emperors from Constantine himself until the 11th century. When the city fell to the Turks in 1453, the church was demolished to make room for the tomb of <a href="/wiki/Mehmet_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Mehmet II">Mehmet II</a> the Conqueror. Justinian was also concerned with other aspects of the city's built environment, legislating against the abuse of laws prohibiting building within 100 feet (30&#160;m) of the sea front, in order to protect the view.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During Justinian I's reign, the city's population reached about 500,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> However, the social fabric of Constantinople was also damaged by the onset of the <a href="/wiki/Plague_of_Justinian" title="Plague of Justinian">Plague of Justinian</a> between 541–542 AD. It killed perhaps 40% of the city's inhabitants.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG/220px-Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG/330px-Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG/440px-Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="1080" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Restored section of the fortifications that protected Constantinople during the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval period">medieval period</a>.</div></div></div>
<h3><span id="Survival.2C_565.E2.80.93717:_Constantinople_during_the_Byzantine_Dark_Ages"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Survival,_565–717:_Constantinople_during_the_Byzantine_Dark_Ages">Survival, 565–717: Constantinople during the Byzantine Dark Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Survival, 565–717: Constantinople during the Byzantine Dark Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the early 7th century, the <a href="/wiki/Avars_(Carpathians)" class="mw-redirect" title="Avars (Carpathians)">Avars</a> and later the <a href="/wiki/Bulgars" title="Bulgars">Bulgars</a> overwhelmed much of the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>, threatening Constantinople with attack from the west. Simultaneously, the <a href="/wiki/Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Persia">Persian</a> <a href="/wiki/Sassanid" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanid">Sassanids</a> overwhelmed the Prefecture of the East and penetrated deep into <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>. <a href="/wiki/Heraclius" title="Heraclius">Heraclius</a>, son of the <a href="/wiki/Exarch" title="Exarch">exarch</a> of Africa, set sail for the city and assumed the throne. He found the military situation so dire that he is said to have contemplated withdrawing the imperial capital to Carthage, but relented after the people of Constantinople begged him to stay. The citizens lost their right to free grain in 618 when Heraclius realised that the city could no longer be supplied from Egypt as a result of the Persian wars: the population fell substantially as a result.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While the city withstood a <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(626)" title="Siege of Constantinople (626)">siege</a> by the Sassanids and Avars in 626, Heraclius campaigned deep into Persian territory and briefly restored the <i>status quo</i> in 628, when the Persians surrendered all their conquests. However, further sieges followed the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Arab_Wars" class="mw-redirect" title="Byzantine–Arab Wars">Arab conquests</a>, first from <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(674%E2%80%93678)" title="Siege of Constantinople (674–678)">674 to 678</a> and then in <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(717%E2%80%93718)" title="Siege of Constantinople (717–718)">717 to 718</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Theodosian_Walls" class="mw-redirect" title="Theodosian Walls">Theodosian Walls</a> kept the city impregnable from the land, while a newly discovered incendiary substance known as <a href="/wiki/Greek_Fire" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Fire">Greek Fire</a> allowed the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_navy" title="Byzantine navy">Byzantine navy</a> to destroy the Arab fleets and keep the city supplied. In the second siege, the second ruler of <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tervel_of_Bulgaria" title="Tervel of Bulgaria">Khan Tervel</a>, rendered decisive help. He was called <i>Saviour of Europe</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="717.E2.80.931025:_Constantinople_during_the_Macedonian_Renaissance"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="717–1025:_Constantinople_during_the_Macedonian_Renaissance">717–1025: Constantinople during the Macedonian Renaissance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 717–1025: Constantinople during the Macedonian Renaissance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Imperial_Gate_mosaic_in_Hagia_Sophia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Imperial_Gate_mosaic_in_Hagia_Sophia.jpg/220px-Imperial_Gate_mosaic_in_Hagia_Sophia.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Imperial_Gate_mosaic_in_Hagia_Sophia.jpg/330px-Imperial_Gate_mosaic_in_Hagia_Sophia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Imperial_Gate_mosaic_in_Hagia_Sophia.jpg/440px-Imperial_Gate_mosaic_in_Hagia_Sophia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2048" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Imperial_Gate_mosaic_in_Hagia_Sophia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Emperor_Leo_VI" class="mw-redirect" title="Emperor Leo VI">Emperor Leo VI</a> (886–912) adoring <a href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesus Christ">Jesus Christ</a>. <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">Mosaic</a> above the Imperial Gate in the <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In the 730s <a href="/wiki/Leo_III_the_Isaurian" title="Leo III the Isaurian">Leo III</a> carried out extensive repairs of the Theodosian walls, which had been damaged by frequent and violent attacks; this work was financed by a special tax on all the subjects of the Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Theodora, widow of the Emperor <a href="/wiki/Theophilos_(emperor)" title="Theophilos (emperor)">Theophilus</a> (died 842), acted as regent during the minority of her son <a href="/wiki/Michael_III" title="Michael III">Michael III</a>, who was said to have been introduced to dissolute habits by her brother Bardas. When Michael assumed power in 856, he became known for excessive drunkenness, appeared in the hippodrome as a charioteer and burlesqued the religious processions of the clergy. He removed Theodora from the Great Palace to the Carian Palace and later to the <a href="/wiki/Sancaktar_Hayrettin_Mosque" title="Sancaktar Hayrettin Mosque">monastery of Gastria</a>, but, after the death of Bardas, she was released to live in the palace of St Mamas; she also had a rural residence at the Anthemian Palace, where Michael was assassinated in 867.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 860, an <a href="/wiki/Rus%27%E2%80%93Byzantine_War_(860)" class="mw-redirect" title="Rus&#39;–Byzantine War (860)">attack</a> was made on the city by a new principality set up a few years earlier at <a href="/wiki/Kiev" title="Kiev">Kiev</a> by <a href="/wiki/Askold_and_Dir" class="mw-redirect" title="Askold and Dir">Askold and Dir</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Varangian" class="mw-redirect" title="Varangian">Varangian</a> chiefs: Two hundred small vessels passed through the Bosporus and plundered the monasteries and other properties on the suburban <a href="/wiki/Prince%27s_Islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince&#39;s Islands">Prince's Islands</a>. <a href="/wiki/Niketas_Oryphas" class="mw-redirect" title="Niketas Oryphas">Oryphas</a>, the admiral of the Byzantine fleet, alerted the emperor Michael, who promptly put the invaders to flight; but the suddenness and savagery of the onslaught made a deep impression on the citizens.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 980, the emperor <a href="/wiki/Basil_II" title="Basil II">Basil II</a> received an unusual gift from Prince <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_I_of_Kiev" class="mw-redirect" title="Vladimir I of Kiev">Vladimir</a> of Kiev: 6,000 <a href="/wiki/Varangian" class="mw-redirect" title="Varangian">Varangian</a> warriors, which Basil formed into a new bodyguard known as the <a href="/wiki/Varangian_Guard" title="Varangian Guard">Varangian Guard</a>. They were known for their ferocity, honour, and loyalty. It is said that, in 1038, they were dispersed in winter quarters in the <a href="/wiki/Thracesian_Theme" title="Thracesian Theme">Thracesian Theme</a> when one of their number attempted to violate a countrywoman, but in the struggle she seized his sword and killed him; instead of taking revenge, however, his comrades applauded her conduct, compensated her with all his possessions, and exposed his body without burial as if he had committed suicide.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> However, following the death of an Emperor, they became known also for plunder in the Imperial palaces.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Later in the 11th Century the Varangian Guard became dominated by <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxons</a> who preferred this way of life to subjugation by the <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_England" title="Norman conquest of England">new Norman kings of England</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_the_Eparch" class="mw-redirect" title="Book of the Eparch">Book of the Eparch</a></i>, which dates to the 10th century, gives a detailed picture of the city's commercial life and its organization at that time. The corporations in which the tradesmen of Constantinople were organised were supervised by the Eparch, who regulated such matters as production, prices, import, and export. Each guild had its own monopoly, and tradesmen might not belong to more than one. It is an impressive testament to the strength of tradition how little these arrangements had changed since the office, then known by the Latin version of its title, had been set up in 330 to mirror the urban prefecture of Rome.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 9th and 10th centuries, Constantinople had a population of between 500,000 and 800,000.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Iconoclast_controversy_in_Constantinople">Iconoclast controversy in Constantinople</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Iconoclast controversy in Constantinople">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the 8th and 9th centuries, the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Iconoclasm" title="Byzantine Iconoclasm">iconoclast</a> movement caused serious political unrest throughout the Empire. The emperor <a href="/wiki/Leo_III_the_Isaurian" title="Leo III the Isaurian">Leo III</a> issued a decree in 726 against images, and ordered the destruction of a statue of Christ over one of the doors of the Chalke, an act that was fiercely resisted by the citizens.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Constantine_V" title="Constantine V">Constantine V</a> convoked a <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Hieria" title="Council of Hieria">church council in 754</a>, which condemned the worship of images, after which many treasures were broken, burned, or painted over with depictions of trees, birds or animals: One source refers to the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_St._Mary_of_Blachernae_(Istanbul)" title="Church of St. Mary of Blachernae (Istanbul)">church of the Holy Virgin</a> at <a href="/wiki/Blachernae" title="Blachernae">Blachernae</a> as having been transformed into a "fruit store and aviary".<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Following the death of her son <a href="/wiki/Leo_IV_the_Khazar" title="Leo IV the Khazar">Leo IV</a> in 780, the empress <a href="/wiki/Irene_(empress)" class="mw-redirect" title="Irene (empress)">Irene</a> restored the veneration of images through the agency of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Nicaea" title="Second Council of Nicaea">Second Council of Nicaea</a> in 787.
</p><p>The iconoclast controversy returned in the early 9th century, only to be resolved once more in 843 during the regency of Empress <a href="/wiki/Theodora_(9th_century)" class="mw-redirect" title="Theodora (9th century)">Theodora</a>, who restored the icons. These controversies contributed to the deterioration of relations between the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic">Western</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern</a> Churches.
</p>
<h3><span id="1025.E2.80.931081:_Constantinople_after_Basil_II"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1025–1081:_Constantinople_after_Basil_II">1025–1081: Constantinople after Basil II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: 1025–1081: Constantinople after Basil II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the late 11th century catastrophe struck with the unexpected and calamitous defeat of the imperial armies at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Manzikert" title="Battle of Manzikert">Battle of Manzikert</a> in Armenia in 1071. The Emperor <a href="/wiki/Romanos_IV" class="mw-redirect" title="Romanos IV">Romanus</a> Diogenes was captured. The peace terms demanded by <a href="/wiki/Alp_Arslan" title="Alp Arslan">Alp Arslan</a>, sultan of the Seljuk Turks, were not excessive, and Romanus accepted them. On his release, however, Romanus found that enemies had placed their own candidate on the throne in his absence; he surrendered to them and suffered death by torture, and the new ruler, <a href="/wiki/Michael_VII" class="mw-redirect" title="Michael VII">Michael VII</a> Ducas, refused to honour the treaty. In response, the Turks began to move into Anatolia in 1073. The collapse of the old defensive system meant that they met no opposition, and the empire's resources were distracted and squandered in a series of civil wars. Thousands of <a href="/wiki/Turkmen_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Turkmen people">Turkoman</a> tribesmen crossed the unguarded frontier and moved into Anatolia. By 1080, a huge area had been lost to the Empire, and the Turks were within striking distance of Constantinople.
</p>
<h3><span id="1081.E2.80.931185:_Constantinople_under_the_Comneni"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1081–1185:_Constantinople_under_the_Comneni">1081–1185: Constantinople under the Comneni</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: 1081–1185: Constantinople under the Comneni">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Byzantium@1180.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Byzantium%401180.jpg/220px-Byzantium%401180.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="102" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Byzantium%401180.jpg/330px-Byzantium%401180.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Byzantium%401180.jpg/440px-Byzantium%401180.jpg 2x" data-file-width="801" data-file-height="370" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Byzantium@1180.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> under <a href="/wiki/Manuel_I_Komnenos" title="Manuel I Komnenos">Manuel I</a>, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1180</span>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Istanbul_2009_Comnenus_Mosaics.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Istanbul_2009_Comnenus_Mosaics.JPG/220px-Istanbul_2009_Comnenus_Mosaics.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Istanbul_2009_Comnenus_Mosaics.JPG/330px-Istanbul_2009_Comnenus_Mosaics.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Istanbul_2009_Comnenus_Mosaics.JPG/440px-Istanbul_2009_Comnenus_Mosaics.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3406" data-file-height="2120" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Istanbul_2009_Comnenus_Mosaics.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>12th century <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">mosaic</a> from the upper gallery of the <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a>, Constantinople. Emperor <a href="/wiki/John_II_Komnenos" title="John II Komnenos">John II</a> (1118–1143) is shown on the left, with the <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Mother_of_Jesus" class="mw-redirect" title="Mary, Mother of Jesus">Virgin Mary</a> and infant <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> in the centre, and John's consort <a href="/wiki/Piroska_of_Hungary" class="mw-redirect" title="Piroska of Hungary">Empress Irene</a> on the right.</div></div></div>
<p>Under the Comnenian dynasty (1081–1185), Byzantium staged a remarkable recovery. In 1090–91, the nomadic <a href="/wiki/Pechenegs" title="Pechenegs">Pechenegs</a> reached the walls of Constantinople, where Emperor Alexius I with the aid of the <a href="/wiki/Kipchaks" title="Kipchaks">Kipchaks</a> annihilated their army.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> In response to a call for aid from <a href="/wiki/Alexios_I_Komnenos" title="Alexios I Komnenos">Alexius</a>, the <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">First Crusade</a> assembled at Constantinople in 1096, but declining to put itself under Byzantine command set out for <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a> on its own account.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_II_Komnenos" title="John II Komnenos">John II</a> built the monastery of the Pantocrator (Almighty) with a hospital for the poor of 50 beds.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With the restoration of firm central government, the empire became fabulously wealthy. The population was rising (estimates for Constantinople in the 12th century vary from some 100,000 to 500,000), and towns and cities across the realm flourished. Meanwhile, the volume of money in circulation dramatically increased. This was reflected in Constantinople by the construction of the Blachernae palace, the creation of brilliant new works of art, and general prosperity at this time: an increase in trade, made possible by the growth of the Italian city-states, may have helped the growth of the economy. It is certain that the <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venetians</a> and others were active traders in Constantinople, making a living out of shipping goods between the Crusader Kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Outremer" class="mw-redirect" title="Outremer">Outremer</a> and the West, while also trading extensively with Byzantium and <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>. The Venetians had factories on the north side of the Golden Horn, and large numbers of westerners were present in the city throughout the 12th century. Toward the end of <a href="/wiki/Manuel_I_Komnenos" title="Manuel I Komnenos">Manuel I Komnenos</a>'s reign, the number of foreigners in the city reached about 60,000–80,000 people out of a total population of about 400,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-popu_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-popu-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> In 1171, Constantinople also contained a small community of 2,500 Jews.<sup id="cite_ref-Jews_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jews-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> In 1182, all Latin (Western European) inhabitants of Constantinople <a href="/wiki/Massacre_of_the_Latins" title="Massacre of the Latins">were massacred</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In artistic terms, the 12th century was a very productive period. There was a revival in the <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">mosaic</a> art, for example: Mosaics became more realistic and vivid, with an increased emphasis on depicting three-dimensional forms. There was an increased demand for art, with more people having access to the necessary wealth to commission and pay for such work. According to N.H. Baynes (<i>Byzantium, An Introduction to East Roman Civilization</i>):
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>With its love of luxury and passion for colour, the art of this age delighted in the production of masterpieces that spread the fame of Byzantium throughout the whole of the Christian world. Beautiful silks from the workshops of Constantinople also portrayed in dazzling colour animals – lions, elephants, eagles, and griffins – confronting each other, or represented Emperors gorgeously arrayed on horseback or engaged in the chase.
</p><p>From the tenth to the twelfth century Byzantium was the main source of inspiration for the West. By their style, arrangement, and iconography the mosaics of St. Mark's at Venice and of the cathedral at <a href="/wiki/Torcello" title="Torcello">Torcello</a> clearly reveal their Byzantine origin. Similarly those of the <a href="/wiki/Cappella_Palatina" title="Cappella Palatina">Palatine Chapel</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Martorana" title="Martorana">Martorana</a> at <a href="/wiki/Palermo" title="Palermo">Palermo</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_Cefal%C3%B9" class="mw-redirect" title="Cathedral of Cefalù">cathedral of Cefalù</a>, together with the vast decoration of the cathedral at Monreale, demonstrate the influence of Byzantium on the <a href="/wiki/Italo-Normans" title="Italo-Normans">Norman</a> Court of <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> in the twelfth century.
