Hispano-<a href="/wiki/Moorish" class="mw-redirect" title="Moorish">Moorish</a> art was unquestionably derived from the Byzantine. <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_art" title="Romanesque art">Romanesque art</a> owes much to the East, from which it borrowed not only its decorative forms but the plan of some of its buildings, as is proved, for instance, by the domed churches of south-western France. Princes of <a href="/wiki/Kiev" title="Kiev">Kiev</a>, <a href="/wiki/Doge_of_Venice" title="Doge of Venice">Venetian doges</a>, abbots of <a href="/wiki/Monte_Cassino" title="Monte Cassino">Monte Cassino</a>, merchants of <a href="/wiki/Amalfi" title="Amalfi">Amalfi</a>, and the kings of Sicily all looked to Byzantium for artists or works of art. Such was the influence of Byzantine art in the twelfth century, that Russia, Venice, southern Italy and Sicily all virtually became provincial centres dedicated to its production."
</p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span id="1185.E2.80.931261:_Constantinople_during_the_Imperial_Exile"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1185–1261:_Constantinople_during_the_Imperial_Exile">1185–1261: Constantinople during the Imperial Exile</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: 1185–1261: Constantinople during the Imperial Exile">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_012.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_012.jpg/220px-Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_012.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_012.jpg/330px-Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_012.jpg/440px-Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_012.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2223" data-file-height="1820" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_012.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Entry of the <a href="/wiki/Crusade" class="mw-redirect" title="Crusade">Crusaders</a> into Constantinople</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix" title="Eugène Delacroix">Eugène Delacroix</a>, 1840.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Byzantium1204.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Byzantium1204.png/220px-Byzantium1204.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Byzantium1204.png/330px-Byzantium1204.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Byzantium1204.png/440px-Byzantium1204.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="357" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Byzantium1204.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Latin_Empire" title="Latin Empire">Latin Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Nicaea" title="Empire of Nicaea">Empire of Nicaea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Trebizond" title="Empire of Trebizond">Empire of Trebizond</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Despotate_of_Epirus" title="Despotate of Epirus">Despotate of Epirus</a>. The borders are very uncertain.</div></div></div>
<p>On 25 July 1197, Constantinople was struck by a <a href="/wiki/Constantine_stilbes#Works" class="mw-redirect" title="Constantine stilbes">severe fire</a> which burned the Latin Quarter and the area around the Gate of the Droungarios (<a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a>: <i lang="tr">Odun Kapısı</i>) on the Golden Horn.<sup id="cite_ref-stilbes_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stilbes-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, the destruction wrought by the 1197 fire paled in comparison with that brought by the Crusaders. In the course of a plot between <a href="/wiki/Philip_of_Swabia" title="Philip of Swabia">Philip of Swabia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boniface_of_Montferrat" class="mw-redirect" title="Boniface of Montferrat">Boniface of Montferrat</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Enrico_Dandolo" title="Enrico Dandolo">Doge of Venice</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Crusade" title="Fourth Crusade">Fourth Crusade</a> was, despite papal excommunication, diverted in 1203 against Constantinople, ostensibly promoting the claims of Alexius, son of the deposed emperor Isaac. The reigning emperor Alexius III had made no preparation. The Crusaders occupied <a href="/wiki/Galata" title="Galata">Galata</a>, broke the <a href="/wiki/Boom_(navigational_barrier)" title="Boom (navigational barrier)">defensive chain</a> protecting the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Horn" title="Golden Horn">Golden Horn</a>, and entered the harbour, where on 27 July they breached the sea walls: Alexius III fled. But the new <a href="/wiki/Alexius_IV" class="mw-redirect" title="Alexius IV">Alexius IV</a> found the Treasury inadequate, and was unable to make good the rewards he had promised to his western allies. Tension between the citizens and the Latin soldiers increased. In January 1204, the <i><a href="/wiki/Protovestiarius" class="mw-redirect" title="Protovestiarius">protovestiarius</a></i> Alexius Murzuphlus provoked a riot, it is presumed, to intimidate Alexius IV, but whose only result was the destruction of the great statue of Athena, the work of <a href="/wiki/Phidias" title="Phidias">Phidias</a>, which stood in the principal forum facing west.
</p><p>In February 1204, the people rose again: Alexius IV was imprisoned and executed, and Murzuphlus took the purple as <a href="/wiki/Alexius_V" class="mw-redirect" title="Alexius V">Alexius V</a>. He made some attempt to repair the walls and organise the citizenry, but there had been no opportunity to bring in troops from the provinces and the guards were demoralised by the revolution. An attack by the Crusaders on 6 April failed, but a second from the Golden Horn on 12 April succeeded, and the invaders poured in. Alexius V fled. The Senate met in Hagia Sophia and offered the crown to Theodore Lascaris, who had married into the Angelid family, but it was too late. He came out with the Patriarch to the <a href="/wiki/Milion" title="Milion">Golden Milestone</a> before the Great Palace and addressed the Varangian Guard. Then the two of them slipped away with many of the nobility and embarked for Asia. By the next day the Doge and the leading Franks were installed in the Great Palace, and the city was given over to pillage for three days.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sir_Steven_Runciman" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Steven Runciman">Sir Steven Runciman</a>, historian of the Crusades, wrote that the sack of Constantinople is "unparalleled in history".
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>For nine centuries, [...] the great city had been the capital of Christian civilisation. It was filled with works of art that had survived from ancient Greece and with the masterpieces of its own exquisite craftsmen. The Venetians [...] seized treasures and carried them off to adorn [...] their town. But the Frenchmen and Flemings were filled with a lust for destruction. They rushed in a howling mob down the streets and through the houses, snatching up everything that glittered and destroying whatever they could not carry, pausing only to murder or to rape, or to break open the wine-cellars [...] . Neither monasteries nor churches nor libraries were spared. In Hagia Sophia itself, drunken soldiers could be seen tearing down the silken hangings and pulling the great silver <a href="/wiki/Iconostasis" title="Iconostasis">iconostasis</a> to pieces, while sacred books and icons were trampled under foot. While they drank merrily from the altar-vessels a prostitute set herself on the Patriarch's throne and began to sing a ribald French song. Nuns were ravished in their convents. Palaces and hovels alike were entered and wrecked. Wounded women and children lay dying in the streets. For three days the ghastly scenes [...] continued, till the huge and beautiful city was a shambles. [...] When [...] order was restored, [...] citizens were tortured to make them reveal the goods that they had contrived to hide.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>For the next half-century, Constantinople was the seat of the <a href="/wiki/Latin_Empire" title="Latin Empire">Latin Empire</a>. Under the rulers of the Latin Empire, the city declined, both in population and the condition of its buildings. Alice-Mary Talbot cites an estimated population for Constantinople of 400,000 inhabitants; after the destruction wrought by the Crusaders on the city, about one third were homeless, and numerous courtiers, nobility, and higher clergy, followed various leading personages into exile. "As a result Constantinople became seriously depopulated," Talbot concludes.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Latins took over at least 20 churches and 13 monasteries, most prominently the Hagia Sophia, which became the cathedral of the Latin Patriarch of Constantinople. It is to these that E.H. Swift attributed the construction of a series of flying buttresses to shore up the walls of the church, which had been weakened over the centuries by earthquake tremors.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> However, this act of maintenance is an exception: for the most part, the Latin occupiers were too few to maintain all of the buildings, either secular and sacred, and many became targets for vandalism or dismantling. Bronze and lead were removed from the roofs of abandoned buildings and melted down and sold to provide money to the chronically under-funded Empire for defense and to support the court; Deno John Geanokoplos writes that "it may well be that a division is suggested here: Latin laymen stripped secular buildings, ecclesiastics, the churches."<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Buildings were not the only targets of officials looking to raise funds for the impoverished Latin Empire: the monumental sculptures which adorned the Hippodrome and fora of the city were pulled down and melted for coinage. "Among the masterpieces destroyed, writes Talbot, "were a Herakles attributed to the fourth-century B.C. sculptor <a href="/wiki/Lysippos" title="Lysippos">Lysippos</a>, and monumental figures of Hera, Paris, and Helen."<sup id="cite_ref-Talbot-248_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Talbot-248-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Nicaean emperor <a href="/wiki/John_III_Vatatzes" class="mw-redirect" title="John III Vatatzes">John III Vatatzes</a> reportedly saved several churches from being dismantled for their valuable building materials; by sending money to the Latins "to buy them off" (<i>exonesamenos</i>), he prevented the destruction of several churches.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> According to Talbot, these included the churches of Blachernae, <a href="/wiki/Rouphinianai" title="Rouphinianai">Rouphinianai</a>, and St. Michael at Anaplous. He also granted funds for the restoration of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Holy_Apostles" title="Church of the Holy Apostles">Church of the Holy Apostles</a>, which had been seriously damaged in an earthquake.<sup id="cite_ref-Talbot-248_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Talbot-248-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Byzantine nobility scattered, many going to <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Nicaea" title="Empire of Nicaea">Nicaea</a>, where Theodore Lascaris set up an imperial court, or to <a href="/wiki/Despotate_of_Epirus" title="Despotate of Epirus">Epirus</a>, where Theodore Angelus did the same; others fled to <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Trebizond" title="Empire of Trebizond">Trebizond</a>, where one of the Comneni had already with Georgian support established an independent seat of empire.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Nicaea and Epirus both vied for the imperial title, and tried to recover Constantinople. In 1261, Constantinople was <a href="/wiki/Reconquest_of_Constantinople" class="mw-redirect" title="Reconquest of Constantinople">captured</a> from its last Latin ruler, <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_II_of_Constantinople" class="mw-redirect" title="Baldwin II of Constantinople">Baldwin II</a>, by the forces of the <a href="/wiki/Nicaean_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Nicaean Empire">Nicaean emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Michael_VIII_Palaiologos" title="Michael VIII Palaiologos">Michael VIII Palaiologos</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="1261.E2.80.931453:_Palaiologan_Era_and_the_Fall_of_Constantinople"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1261–1453:_Palaiologan_Era_and_the_Fall_of_Constantinople">1261–1453: Palaiologan Era and the Fall of Constantinople</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: 1261–1453: Palaiologan Era and the Fall of Constantinople">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire_under_the_Palaiologos_dynasty" title="Byzantine Empire under the Palaiologos dynasty">Byzantine Empire under the Palaiologos dynasty</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">Fall of Constantinople</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zonaro_GatesofConst.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Zonaro_GatesofConst.jpg/220px-Zonaro_GatesofConst.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="298" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Zonaro_GatesofConst.jpg/330px-Zonaro_GatesofConst.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Zonaro_GatesofConst.jpg/440px-Zonaro_GatesofConst.jpg 2x" data-file-width="903" data-file-height="1224" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zonaro_GatesofConst.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mehmed_the_Conqueror" title="Mehmed the Conqueror">Mehmed the Conqueror</a> enters Constantinople, painting by <a href="/wiki/Fausto_Zonaro" title="Fausto Zonaro">Fausto Zonaro</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Although Constantinople was retaken by <a href="/wiki/Michael_VIII_Palaiologos" title="Michael VIII Palaiologos">Michael VIII Palaiologos</a>, the Empire had lost many of its key economic resources, and struggled to survive. The <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Blachernae" title="Palace of Blachernae">palace of Blachernae</a> in the north-west of the city became the main Imperial residence, with the old Great Palace on the shores of the <a href="/wiki/Bosporus" title="Bosporus">Bosporus</a> going into decline. When Michael VIII captured the city, its population was 35,000 people, but, by the end of his reign, he had succeeded in increasing the population to about 70,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-popu2_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-popu2-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> The Emperor achieved this by summoning former residents who had fled the city when the crusaders captured it, and by relocating Greeks from the recently reconquered <a href="/wiki/Peloponnese" title="Peloponnese">Peloponnese</a> to the capital.<sup id="cite_ref-repopu_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-repopu-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> In 1347, the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> spread to Constantinople.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> In 1453, when the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turks" title="Ottoman Turks">Ottoman Turks</a> <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">captured the city</a>, it contained approximately 50,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-popu3_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-popu3-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Constantinople was conquered by the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> on 29 May 1453.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> The Ottomans were commanded by 22-year-old Ottoman Sultan Mehmed II. The conquest of Constantinople followed a seven-week siege which had begun on 6 April 1453.
</p>
<h3><span id="1453.E2.80.931922:_Ottoman_Kostantiniyye"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1453–1922:_Ottoman_Kostantiniyye">1453–1922: Ottoman Kostantiniyye</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: 1453–1922: Ottoman Kostantiniyye">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Constantinople_during_the_Ottoman_era" class="mw-redirect" title="Constantinople during the Ottoman era">Constantinople during the Ottoman era</a></div>
<p>The Christian Orthodox city of Constantinople was now under Ottoman control. When <a href="/wiki/Mehmed_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Mehmed II">Mehmed II</a> finally entered Constantinople through what is now known as the <a href="/wiki/Topkapi_Palace" class="mw-redirect" title="Topkapi Palace">Topkapi Gate</a>, he immediately rode his horse to the <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a>, where he ordered his soldiers to stop hacking at the marbles and 'be satisfied with the booty and captives; as for all the buildings, they belonged to him'.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> He ordered that an <a href="/wiki/Imam" title="Imam">imam</a> meet him there in order to chant the <a href="/wiki/Adhan" title="Adhan">adhan</a> thus transforming the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodoxy" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Orthodoxy">Orthodox</a> cathedral into a Muslim <a href="/wiki/Mosque" title="Mosque">mosque</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> solidifying <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islamic</a> rule in Constantinople.
</p><p>Mehmed's main concern with Constantinople had to do with rebuilding the city's defenses and population. Building projects were commenced immediately after the conquest, which included the repair of the walls, construction of the citadel, and building a new palace.<sup id="cite_ref-Inalcik,_Halil_1969,_p._236_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Inalcik,_Halil_1969,_p._236-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Mehmed issued orders across his empire that Muslims, Christians, and Jews should resettle the city; he demanded that five thousand households needed to be transferred to Constantinople by September.<sup id="cite_ref-Inalcik,_Halil_1969,_p._236_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Inalcik,_Halil_1969,_p._236-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> From all over the Islamic empire, prisoners of war and deported people were sent to the city: these people were called "Sürgün" in Turkish (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">σουργούνιδες</span>).<sup id="cite_ref-mw28_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mw28-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Two centuries later, Ottoman traveler <a href="/wiki/Evliya_%C3%87elebi" title="Evliya Çelebi">Evliya Çelebi</a> gave a list of groups introduced into the city with their respective origins. Even today, many quarters of <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Aksaray,_Istanbul" class="mw-redirect" title="Aksaray, Istanbul">Aksaray</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%87ar%C5%9Famba,_Istanbul" class="mw-redirect" title="Çarşamba, Istanbul">Çarşamba</a>, bear the names of the places of origin of their inhabitants.<sup id="cite_ref-mw28_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mw28-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> However, many people escaped again from the city, and there were several outbreaks of plague, so that in 1459 Mehmet allowed the deported Greeks to come back to the city.<sup id="cite_ref-mw28_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mw28-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Istanbull_-_palasset_-_13.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Istanbull_-_palasset_-_13.jpg/220px-Istanbull_-_palasset_-_13.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Istanbull_-_palasset_-_13.jpg/330px-Istanbull_-_palasset_-_13.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Istanbull_-_palasset_-_13.jpg/440px-Istanbull_-_palasset_-_13.jpg 2x" data-file-width="668" data-file-height="455" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Istanbull_-_palasset_-_13.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Eagle and Snake</i>, 6th century mosaic flooring Constantinople, <a href="/wiki/Great_Palace_of_Constantinople" title="Great Palace of Constantinople">Grand Imperial Palace</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Constantinople was the largest and richest urban center in the Eastern Mediterranean Sea during the late Eastern Roman Empire, mostly as a result of its strategic position commanding the trade routes between the Aegean Sea and the Black Sea. It would remain the capital of the eastern, Greek-speaking empire for over a thousand years. At its peak, roughly corresponding to the Middle Ages, it was the richest and largest European city, exerting a powerful cultural pull and dominating economic life in the Mediterranean. Visitors and merchants were especially struck by the beautiful monasteries and churches of the city, in particular the <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a>, or the Church of Holy Wisdom. According to Russian 14th-century traveler Stephen of Novgorod: "As for Hagia Sophia, the human mind can neither tell it nor make description of it."
</p><p>It was especially important for preserving in its libraries manuscripts of Greek and Latin authors throughout a period when instability and disorder caused their mass-destruction in western Europe and north Africa: On the city's fall, thousands of these were brought by refugees to Italy, and played a key part in stimulating the Renaissance, and the transition to the modern world. The cumulative influence of the city on the west, over the many centuries of its existence, is incalculable. In terms of technology, art and culture, as well as sheer size, Constantinople was without parallel anywhere in Europe for a thousand years.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Women_in_literature">Women in literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Women in literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Constantinople was home to the first known Western <a href="/wiki/Armenian_literature" title="Armenian literature">Armenian</a> journal published and edited by a woman (Elpis Kesaratsian). Entering circulation in 1862, <i>Kit'arr</i> or <i>Guitar</i> stayed in print for only 7 months. Female writers who openly expressed their desires were viewed as immodest, but this changed slowly as journals began to publish more "women's sections". In the 1880s, Matteos Mamurian invited <a href="/wiki/Srpouhi_Dussap" title="Srpouhi Dussap">Srpouhi Dussap</a> to submit essays for <i>Arevelian Mamal</i>. According to Zaruhi Galemkearian's autobiography, she was told to write about women's place in the family and home after she published two volumes of poetry in the 1890s. By 1900, several Armenian journals had started to include works by female contributors including the Constantinople-based <i>Tsaghik</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_architecture" title="Byzantine architecture">Byzantine architecture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ayasofya-Innenansicht.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Ayasofya-Innenansicht.jpg/220px-Ayasofya-Innenansicht.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="332" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Ayasofya-Innenansicht.jpg/330px-Ayasofya-Innenansicht.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Ayasofya-Innenansicht.jpg/440px-Ayasofya-Innenansicht.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1090" data-file-height="1646" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ayasofya-Innenansicht.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Interior view of the <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a>, currently a museum.</div></div></div>
<p>The Byzantine Empire used Roman and Greek architectural models and styles to create its own unique type of architecture. The influence of Byzantine architecture and art can be seen in the copies taken from it throughout Europe. Particular examples include <a href="/wiki/St_Mark%27s_Basilica" title="St Mark&#39;s Basilica">St Mark's Basilica</a> in Venice,<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> the basilicas of <a href="/wiki/Ravenna" title="Ravenna">Ravenna</a>, and many churches throughout the Slavic East. Also, alone in Europe until the 13th-century Italian <a href="/wiki/Italian_coin_florin" class="mw-redirect" title="Italian coin florin">florin</a>, the Empire continued to produce sound gold coinage, the <a href="/wiki/Solidus_(coin)" title="Solidus (coin)">solidus</a> of <a href="/wiki/Diocletian" title="Diocletian">Diocletian</a> becoming the <a href="/wiki/Bezant" title="Bezant">bezant</a> prized throughout the Middle Ages. Its <a href="/wiki/Theodosian_Walls" class="mw-redirect" title="Theodosian Walls">city walls</a> were much imitated (for example, see <a href="/wiki/Caernarfon_Castle" title="Caernarfon Castle">Caernarfon Castle</a>) and its urban infrastructure was moreover a marvel throughout the Middle Ages, keeping alive the art, skill and technical expertise of the Roman Empire. In the Ottoman period Islamic architecture and symbolism were used.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Constantine's foundation gave prestige to the Bishop of Constantinople, who eventually came to be known as the <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarch_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarch of Constantinople">Ecumenical Patriarch</a>, and made it a prime center of Christianity alongside Rome. This contributed to cultural and theological differences between Eastern and Western Christianity eventually leading to the <a href="/wiki/East-West_Schism" class="mw-redirect" title="East-West Schism">Great Schism</a> that divided <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Western Catholicism</a> from <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodoxy" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Orthodoxy">Eastern Orthodoxy</a> from 1054 onwards. Constantinople is also of great religious importance to <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, as the conquest of Constantinople is one of the signs of the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_eschatology" title="Islamic eschatology">End time in Islam</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1909, in Constantinople there were 626 primary schools and 12 secondary schools. Of the primary schools 561 were of the lower grade and 65 were of the higher grade; of the latter, 34 were public and 31 were private. There was one secondary college and eleven secondary preparatory schools.<sup id="cite_ref-Reportp570_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reportp570-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" decoding="async" width="20" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31" /></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">July 2019</span>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>

<p>In the past the Bulgarian newspapers in the late Ottoman period were <i>Makedoniya</i>, <i>Napredŭk</i>, and <i>Pravo</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Straussp267_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Straussp267-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Popular_culture">Popular culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Popular culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Schedel_konstantinopel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Schedel_konstantinopel.jpg/220px-Schedel_konstantinopel.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="358" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Schedel_konstantinopel.jpg/330px-Schedel_konstantinopel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Schedel_konstantinopel.jpg/440px-Schedel_konstantinopel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1625" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Schedel_konstantinopel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Page depicting Constantinople in the <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_Chronicle" title="Nuremberg Chronicle">Nuremberg Chronicle</a> published in 1493, forty years after the city's fall to the Muslims.</div></div></div>
<ul><li>Constantinople appears as a city of wondrous majesty, beauty, remoteness, and nostalgia in <a href="/wiki/William_Butler_Yeats" class="mw-redirect" title="William Butler Yeats">William Butler Yeats</a>' 1928 poem "<a href="/wiki/Sailing_to_Byzantium" title="Sailing to Byzantium">Sailing to Byzantium</a>."</li>
<li>Constantinople, as seen under the Byzantine emperor <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_II" title="Theodosius II">Theodosius II</a>, makes several on-screen appearances in the 2001 TV miniseries <i><a href="/wiki/Attila_(TV_miniseries)" class="mw-redirect" title="Attila (TV miniseries)">Attila</a></i> as the capital of the Eastern Roman Empire.</li>
<li>Finnish author <a href="/wiki/Mika_Waltari" title="Mika Waltari">Mika Waltari</a> wrote one of his most-acclaimed historical novels, <i>Johannes Angelos</i> (published in English by name <i>"<a href="/wiki/The_Dark_Angel_(Waltari)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Dark Angel (Waltari)">The Dark Angel</a>"</i>) on the fall of Constantinople.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Robert_Graves" title="Robert Graves">Robert Graves</a>, author of <i><a href="/wiki/I,_Claudius" title="I, Claudius">I, Claudius</a>,</i> also wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Count_Belisarius" title="Count Belisarius">Count Belisarius</a>,</i> a historical novel about <a href="/wiki/Belisarius" title="Belisarius">Belisarius</a>. Graves set much of the novel in the Constantinople of Justinian I.</li>
<li>Constantinople provides the setting of much of the action in <a href="/wiki/Umberto_Eco" title="Umberto Eco">Umberto Eco</a>'s 2000 novel <i><a href="/wiki/Baudolino" title="Baudolino">Baudolino</a>.</i></li>
<li>The name Constantinople was made easy to spell thanks to a novelty song, "C-O-N-S-T-A-N-T-I-N-O-P-L-E," written by Harry Carlton and performed by Paul Whiteman and his Orchestra, in the 1920s.</li>
<li>Constantinople's change of name was the theme for a song made famous by <a href="/wiki/The_Four_Lads" title="The Four Lads">The Four Lads</a> and later covered by <a href="/wiki/They_Might_Be_Giants" title="They Might Be Giants">They Might Be Giants</a> and many others, titled "<a href="/wiki/Istanbul_(Not_Constantinople)" title="Istanbul (Not Constantinople)">Istanbul (Not Constantinople)</a>."</li>
<li>"Constantinople" was one of the "big words" the Father knows toward the end of <a href="/wiki/Dr._Seuss" title="Dr. Seuss">Dr. Seuss</a>'s book, <i><a href="/wiki/Hop_on_Pop" title="Hop on Pop">Hop on Pop</a>.</i> (The other was Timbuktu.)</li>
<li>"Constantinople" was also the title of the opening edit of <a href="/wiki/The_Residents" title="The Residents">The Residents</a>' <a href="/wiki/Extended_play" title="Extended play">EP</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Duck_Stab/Buster_%26_Glen" title="Duck Stab/Buster &amp; Glen">Duck Stab!</a></i>, released in 1978.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Queen_(band)" title="Queen (band)">Queen</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Roger_Meddows_Taylor" class="mw-redirect" title="Roger Meddows Taylor">Roger Meddows Taylor</a> included the track "Interlude in Constantinople" on Side 2 of his debut album <a href="/wiki/Fun_in_Space" title="Fun in Space">Fun in Space</a>.</li>
<li>A Montreal-based folk/classical/fusion band calls itself "Constantinople."</li>

<li>"Constantinople" is the title of a song by <a href="/wiki/The_Decemberists" title="The Decemberists">The Decemberists</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Stephen_Lawhead" class="mw-redirect" title="Stephen Lawhead">Stephen Lawhead</a>'s novel <i>Byzantium</i> (1996) is set in 9th-century Constantinople.</li>
<li>Folk Metal band <a href="/wiki/Turisas" title="Turisas">Turisas</a> makes multiple references to Constantinople in their song "Miklagard Overture," referring to it as "Konstantinopolis," "Tsargrad," and "Miklagard."</li>
<li>Constantinople makes an appearance in the MMORPG game <i><a href="/wiki/Silkroad_Online" title="Silkroad Online">Silkroad</a></i> as a major capital, along with a major Chinese capital.</li>
<li>Constantinople makes an appearance in the "<a href="/wiki/Rome_Total_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Rome Total War">Rome Total War</a>" expansion "<a href="/wiki/Rome_Total_War:_Barbarian_Invasion" class="mw-redirect" title="Rome Total War: Barbarian Invasion">Barbarian Invasion</a>" belonging to the Eastern Roman Empire. It would reappear in the same role for the spiritual sequel, <a href="/wiki/Total_War:_Attila" title="Total War: Attila">Total War: Attila</a>.</li>
<li>Constantinople also makes an appearance in "<a href="/wiki/Medieval_Total_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval Total War">Medieval Total War</a>." It is a starting province and city of the Byzantines.</li>
<li>Constantinople makes an appearance in the game "<a href="/wiki/Age_of_Empires_II:_The_Age_of_Kings" class="mw-redirect" title="Age of Empires II: The Age of Kings">Age of Empires II: The Age of Kings</a>" in the fifth scenario of the Barbarossa campaign and again in the third scenario of the Attila the Hun campaign in the expansion pack "<a href="/wiki/Age_of_Empires_II:_The_Conquerors_Expansion" class="mw-redirect" title="Age of Empires II: The Conquerors Expansion">Age of Empires II: The Conquerors Expansion</a>."</li>
<li>Constantinople is the main setting of the game "<a href="/wiki/Assassin%27s_Creed:_Revelations" title="Assassin&#39;s Creed: Revelations">Assassin's Creed: Revelations</a>," the fourth major title in the best-selling "<a href="/wiki/Assassin%27s_Creed" title="Assassin&#39;s Creed">Assassin's Creed</a>" series.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Constantinople is also a setting of the Vampire: The Dark Ages role playing game by White Wolf.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Constantinople is one of the territories featured in the <a href="/wiki/Board_Game" class="mw-redirect" title="Board Game">Board Game</a> <a href="/wiki/Diplomacy_(game)" title="Diplomacy (game)">Diplomacy</a>. It is one of the default territories of Turkey.</li>
<li>Constantinople appears in "<a href="/wiki/Europa_Universalis_IV" title="Europa Universalis IV">Europa Universalis IV</a>" and in "<a href="/wiki/Crusader_Kings_II" title="Crusader Kings II">Crusader Kings II</a>" as the capital of the Byzantine Empire, which is featured in both games.</li>
<li>Constantinople appears as the capital of the Byzantine civilization in several installments of the video game series "<a href="/wiki/Civilization_(series)" title="Civilization (series)">Civilization</a>".</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International_status">International status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: International status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Constantinople_imperial_district.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Constantinople_imperial_district.png/220px-Constantinople_imperial_district.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="249" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Constantinople_imperial_district.png/330px-Constantinople_imperial_district.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Constantinople_imperial_district.png/440px-Constantinople_imperial_district.png 2x" data-file-width="717" data-file-height="812" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Constantinople_imperial_district.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Constantinople's monumental center.</div></div></div>
<p>The city provided a defence for the eastern provinces of the old Roman Empire against the barbarian invasions of the 5th century. The 18-meter-tall walls built by <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_II" title="Theodosius II">Theodosius II</a> were, in essence, impregnable to the barbarians coming from south of the <a href="/wiki/Danube_river" class="mw-redirect" title="Danube river">Danube river</a>, who found easier targets to the west rather than the richer provinces to the east in Asia. From the 5th century, the city was also protected by the <a href="/wiki/Anastasian_Wall" title="Anastasian Wall">Anastasian Wall</a>, a 60-kilometer chain of walls across the <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thracian</a> <a href="/wiki/Peninsula" title="Peninsula">peninsula</a>. Many scholars<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (January 2010)">who?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> argue that these sophisticated fortifications allowed the east to develop relatively unmolested while <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Ancient Rome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">the west</a> collapsed.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Constantinople's fame was such that <a href="/wiki/Sino-Roman_relations" title="Sino-Roman relations">it was described</a> even in contemporary <a href="/wiki/Twenty-Four_Histories" title="Twenty-Four Histories">Chinese histories</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Old_Book_of_Tang" title="Old Book of Tang">Old</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/New_Book_of_Tang" title="New Book of Tang">New Book of Tang</a></i>, which mentioned its massive walls and gates as well as a purported <a href="/wiki/Water_clock" title="Water clock">clepsydra</a> mounted with a golden statue of a man.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hirth_2000_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hirth_2000-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> The Chinese histories even related how the city <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(674-678)" class="mw-redirect" title="Siege of Constantinople (674-678)">had been besieged</a> in the 7th century by <a href="/wiki/Muawiyah_I" title="Muawiyah I">Muawiyah I</a> and how he exacted <a href="/wiki/Tribute" title="Tribute">tribute</a> in a peace settlement.<sup id="cite_ref-hirth_2000_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hirth_2000-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Reife_Konstantinopeler_Quitten_aus_dem_Vogelsberg.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Reife_Konstantinopeler_Quitten_aus_dem_Vogelsberg.jpg/220px-Reife_Konstantinopeler_Quitten_aus_dem_Vogelsberg.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="253" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Reife_Konstantinopeler_Quitten_aus_dem_Vogelsberg.jpg/330px-Reife_Konstantinopeler_Quitten_aus_dem_Vogelsberg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Reife_Konstantinopeler_Quitten_aus_dem_Vogelsberg.jpg/440px-Reife_Konstantinopeler_Quitten_aus_dem_Vogelsberg.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2177" data-file-height="2506" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Reife_Konstantinopeler_Quitten_aus_dem_Vogelsberg.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Quince" title="Quince">Constantinople apple quinces</a></div></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-34">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Commemorative coins that were issued during the 330s already refer to the city as <i>Constantinopolis</i> (see, e.g., Michael Grant, <i>The climax of Rome</i> (London 1968), p. 133), or "Constantine's City". According to the <i>Reallexikon für Antike und Christentum</i>, vol. 164 (Stuttgart 2005), column 442, there is no evidence for the tradition that Constantine officially dubbed the city "New Rome" (<i>Nova Roma</i>). It is possible that the Emperor called the city "Second Rome" (<a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">Δευτέρα Ῥώμη</span>, <i>Deutera Rhōmē</i>) by official decree, as reported by the 5th-century church historian <a href="/wiki/Socrates_of_Constantinople" title="Socrates of Constantinople">Socrates of Constantinople</a>: See <a href="/wiki/Names_of_Constantinople" class="mw-redirect" title="Names of Constantinople">Names of Constantinople</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-35">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A description can be found in the <a href="/wiki/Notitia_urbis_Constantinopolitanae" class="mw-redirect" title="Notitia urbis Constantinopolitanae">Notitia urbis Constantinopolitanae</a>.</span>
</li>
